Jewish Center Sisterhood

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

Women’s League for Conservative Judaism has chosen for this year’s
theme, “Ve-he’ yay Berakhah - - And Be Thou a Blessing.” This theme
provides us with direction for the coming year. It contains the ideal which
Abraham was to set for himself which, according to Dr. Joseph Hertz, was
“to become a blessing to humanity by the beneficient influence of God.”
It is this ideal which motivates us to function as role models in our com-
munity, in our homes, in our synagogues and our Sisterhoods, so each of
us may become a blessing.

As we embark on a new administration of our Sisterhood, it is our hope
that we, like Abraham, will succeed in turning the Jewish People to a know-
ledge of God through the strength and commitment of Jewish womanhood.

Through our many programs and projects we plan to cultivate our
religious, educational and cultural life. With your support we can achieve
our goal.

Annetta

 

Annetta Kornblum
President

BRAS R89
NENTS,

a

CENTER SISTERHOOD BOARD

Left to right; Betty Gray, Youth Vice-President; Renee Gordon, Ways
and Means Vice-President; Shirley Bielski, Corresponding Secretary;
Annetta Kornblum, President; Isabel Balotin, Cultural Vice-President;
Gertrude Kramer, Recording Secretary; Kay Schneider, Membership Vice-
President; Horty Gendzier, Financial Secretary. Missing from picture,
Harriet Kaplan, Treasurer.

Outgoing President Ellen Freedman accepts present
from Annetta Kornblum. Ellen’s term of office was
marked with dignity and pride. She worked very
hard to make Sisterhood a cohesive women’s group.
Her intelligence and enthusiasm were prevalent at

all her meetings. We appreciate all her hard work
and thank her husband, Norman and son, Michael

for their support.

===== PAGE 16 =====
ewish Community Council

“Israel has become the principal authority-giving element in Jewish life today.
... Israel appears to have ended a period lasting well over a century during
which there was no clear-cut source of authority in Jewish life.

... The fact that Israel has become the new source of authority represents a

break with tradition . . provides a means for uniting a people with very diverse
beliefs.” (Elazar)

Israel is the force and symbol that most excites Jews today in the United
States and, naturally, in Jacksonville. Since funding for Israel is so important
and high priority, the Jacksonville Jewish Community Council has become the
important and focal organization in Jacksonville. It is the focal point of almost
all the Jewish organizations in the city. Because all other Jewish American com-
munities are included in the above sentiment, the Council is linked through a
somewhat loose organizational network to all aspects of Israeli, national and
regional endeavors.

The continuing Israeli-Arab crisis, emphasized by the wars of 1967 and 1973,

i has served to confirm and establish the realization that not only are Jews bound ce 8
Ted Cohn, President together by religious affiliation, but they are bound together as a people by a Gerald Goldsmith
common fate. Executive Director

The Jacksonville community has begun to realize that in order to be a part of the total Jewish community, it must also develop itself into a
strong and knowledgeable community in Jacksonville. The Council is one of the prime Jewish sources of funds for River Garden and Jewish
Family & Children’s Service. Not only does the Council allocate funds to ADL, but it works in conjunction with the ADL Regional Office in
Miami in stemming anti-Semitism which has reared its head recently in our state.

This past summer the Council, in cooperation with the four synagogues of Jacksonville, has undertaken a survey of the conditions of Jewish
education in Jacksonville. The survey has been published and the Council has already allocated a significant sum to be used when proper solu-
tions and recommendations are adopted by all concerned.

The Council has a program, based on anational guidelines, for the development of the future leaders of the Jacksonville community. Men
and women from all synagogues and organizations recommend those whom they wish for this training, so that a continuity of competent and
interested leaders in all phases of Jewish life will carry on the community’s work.

The Women’s Council of Presidents and the Women’s Educational Program that the Council sponsors, ensures both harmony and en-
thused, well-informed leaders in our community.

Through Council allocation, a number of Jewish Russian families have been brought in to Jacksonville. This has been a new endeavor and
the Council has collected all its resources to make this project a smooth-running and more successful endeavor than ever in the future.

One definition of Judaism includes the phrase . . . “and asense of peoplehood” . .. The members of the Jewish community in Jacksonville,
through the Jewish Community Council and the host of Jewish organizations, are developing a sense of unity and of responsibility for the
physical and spiritual welfare of the Jewish people locally and for all other Jews wherever they may be. E. Theodore Cohn, President

===== PAGE 18 =====
Jewish Center Sisterhood

Sisterhood Bat Mitzuah Class

The class was designed by Rabbi Gaffney
to provide a basic Jewish Education for
adults who want to continue studying
Jewish life, regardless of whether or not
they had celebrated a Bat Mitzvah cere-
mony during their adolescent years.

In progress since January, the program
focuses on Jewish History, Jewish Life,
the Prayerbook. Jewish Liturgy as well as
Jewish thoughts and personalities are dis-
cussed and analyzed. In the spring of 1979
the class will conclude with a fitting cere-
mony. The enthusiasm of the ladies and
Rabbi is prevalent at each Tuesday morn-
ing session.

 

Seated front to back, left to right; Carolyn Krestul, Shirley Kollin, Judy Silverman, Isabel
Balotin, Shirley Bielski, Clara Mann, Rabbi Gaffney, Bea Browdy, Wendy Honigman, Judy

Mizrahi, Hannah Pomeranz, Ella Kleiman, Roz Klein, Rose Harris. Missing from picture;
Rhoda Goldstein, Ellen Shamah.

“Friendship”

Friendship is a priceless gift

that cannot be bought or sold,
But its value is far greater

than a mountain made of gold —
For gold is cold and lifeless,

it can neither see nor hear,
And in time of trouble

it is powerless to cheer —
It has no ears to listen,

no heart to understand.
It cannot bring you comfort

or reach out a helping hand —
So when you ask God for a gift,

be thankful if He sends

Not diamonds, pearls or riches,

FLORIDA BRANCH CONVENTION but the love of real true friends.

Florida Branch Women’s League for Conservative
Judaism held its Annual Convention in Miami Beach
May 3-5. Those Sisterhood members who attended
were left to right; Clara Mann, Betty Gray, Annetta
Kornblum, Shirley Bielski, Marsha Pollock, Kay
Schneider and Isabel Balotin. This convention was
very special for us as Marsha Pollock was installed
as Vice-President of Florida Branch.

COMMUNITY

Hillel said, “If I am not for myself, who will I be?

What are the 3
most widely held securities
in the United States?

U. S. Savings Bonds,
A.T.&T....and
STATE OF ISRAEL BONDS!

 

You can't argue with success, as the saying goes. And Israel Bonds
have had a lot of success since their inception in 1951. Over 350
of the largest banks and financial institutions in the United States own
Israel Bonds. Almost $4 billion worth have been sold, of which $2 billion
has been repaid as they came due.

otate of Israel bond money never leaves the United States. The
proceeds remain here in the form of bank credits, permitting Israeli
industrialists and agricultural experts to purchase equipment and
machinery to create jobs for newcomers to Israel, who are arriving
by the hundreds each week.

There are dozens of ways you can use aae bonds other
than in your investment portfolio .
Gifts to Universities: every major eollege in the United States has
accepted Israel Bonds. If you choose Harvard, they’ll match your gift
with an equal Israel Bonds purchase.

Use Israel bonds instead of cash for travel in Israel.

Pension Plans: Many pension fund and Keogh Plan retirement funds
use Israel Bonds as investments.

A reception honoring Mr. Harry Gendzier will be held on October
29th, 1978. The investment potential of Israel Bonds will be discussed
and a prominent speaker will be featured. Plan on attending!!

===== PAGE 24 =====
A’RAYIM

Ha’Rayim is the young family fellowship of the
Jacksonville Jewish Center. It has been in exis-
tence now for over three years. Ha’Rayim (the
friends) is based on the principle that religious and
intellectual expression can best take place in a
social context.

Last August, Ha’Rayim hosted a Wine and Cheese
Party for newcomers. It was a great success and
many new friendships were made. In September
we sponsored a Congregational Get-together on
Selihot evening. It featured a creative Havdalah
ceremony, followed by a social hour, dancing,
discussion and Selihot Service. The entire evening
was beautiful and inspiring and was an excellent
prelude to the High Holiday season.

Other successful functions were the Bonfire and
Cookout, and Disco at 10101. The purpose of the
group is not to raise funds, but merely a way for
Jewish couples to get together and share Jewish
experiences in a friendly, social and relaxed atmos-
phere.

Many exciting plans are being made for this year.
Watch your bulletin, and join us.

Through its programs the Jewish Center seeks to foster individual growth and deve-
lopment, encourage participation in community life, provide opportunity for friend-
ship, fun and the acquisition and expression of Jewish knowledge and identification.
To achieve these objectives, the Jewish Center employs the services of professionally-
trained staff as well as experts ina variety of related fields. The Annual Sunday Fun-
day Program brings all Jewish adults and children of the community together in the
friendly atmosphere of the Jewish Center. The Center’s 36 acre complex contains full
and comprehensive facilities for cultural arts, community forums, social, athletic
and club activities.

Other classes not pictured were; Assertive Living, Art, Baton and Majorette, Cheer-
leading, Self-Defense, Slimnastics, Theatre Workshop, Self Hypnosis and Racquet-
ball. Co-ordinator of the Sunday Funday Program was Shelly Kulchin. Assisting
Lemon Clay, Jr. as basketball coach were; Robert Cantor, Alan Chepenik, Gary
Fletcher, Gene Kornblum, Jeff Morris, Jack Schemer, George Strumlauf and Steve
Strumlauf. THANKS GUYS!!!

L to R; Past President, Ruth Slutzah, Joanne Davis, Honey Donziger, Elaine
Roberts, Lenore Michelson, Sybil Ansbacher, Eileen Kaufman.

National Council of Jewish Women is an organization which, in the spirit of Judaism, is dedicated to furthering human welfare in the Jewish
and general community, locally, nationally, and internationally.

Our community service projects include: Pre-school vision testing to discover sight problems in the young child; service to foreign born; the
“Art Enrichment Program” which trains volunteers at the Art Museum to bring and discuss art in the public and private schools. The Mental
Health Forum, in coalition with the Mental Health Association, which attracts over 2000 participants yearly; and a film of the Mock Trial,
“Pursuit of Privacy”, produced by our Section is available for educational use through the Haydon Burns Library.

In Israel, National Council of Jewish Women’s Educational Research Center is an innovative program for education of the disadvantaged.
Fellowships for Israeli teachers and social workers who wish to study in our country and the continued maintenance of NCJW’s Hebrew
University High School are all projects which are helping to educate Israel’s youth.

NCJW’s legislative group, “Talk and Tour” and our advocacy programs have been educational for our members and effective in promoting
Council’s concerns on a national, state and local level. Members have been active in coalition with League of Women Voters in a successful
voter registration project.

A trip to Tallahassee during the legislative session is available to all members and puts into action many of our priorities as well as being an
exhilarating experience.

Our Regular Membership Meetings are the third Wednesday of the month. The programs are:
Sept. 20, 1978 - Brunch with Dr. Sheldon Kaplan, Guest Speaker
Oct. 18, 1978 - Evening meeting with former State Senator Jack Matthews discussing Constitutional Revision
Nov. 15, 1978 - Luncheon for Paid Up Members, Fashions by Clothes Collection
Nov. 26, 1978 - Bargainata
Jan. 17, 1979 - Luncheon, “Women in Power”
March 21, 1979 - “Council goes to The Movies”
April 26, 1979 - Mental Health Forum
May 16, 1979 - Installation

The Henry Kramer Library and Resource Center is being newly furnished and equipped to promote library
services for the entire congregation. Through the generosity of Mrs. Henry Kramer, this vital and important
unit of the Center will be beautifully furnished. The facilities include the Tofield Library, the Solomon Schechter
Day School Room and a reading and browsing area.

Many generations to come will derive a great deal of pleasure and knowledge from the books that fill the
library shelves. College students, professors and high school students all utilize this facility. We are proud of the
most comprehensive collection of Judaic literature in Northeast Florida.

The Library has been manned in the interim period by Mrs. Bernard Glossman, Mrs. Philip Selber, Mrs. Sidney
Stillman and Mrs. Milton Mann.

Members of the former Tofield Memorial Library Committee have been instrumental in the change over
process.

Calendar of Activities:

3rd Monday of each month - Admissions Board - 8:00 P.M.
4th Monday of each month - Board of Trustees - 8:00 P.M.
Anniversary Day - March 25, 1978, 2-5 P.M.

By a coincidence of the calendar, this year’s anniversary day, March 25, 1978 was the same Sunday and date of the month
when River Garden held its first Open House March 25, 1946. Since that date, there has been continuing progress in meeting
the needs of the Jewish Communities of Florida. From a colonial mansion housing 12 residents to a modern, specially
designed facility serving 190 frail and elderly reflects the concerns and interests of our people in observing the Fifth
Commandment “Honor thy Father and Mother... ”

In an era, when the miracles of medical sciences has extended life for many for beyond the three score and ten, River
Garden anticipated the needs of those elderly who might require a protected environment and provided these services in a
home like atmosphere. There are many problems in provided these services in a home like atmosphere. There are many
problems in providing medical, nursing, dietary and other services essential to the basic activities for daily living for 190
residents on a 24 hour basis for 365 days in the year. All too often, the individual gets lost in the mechanics of providing
services and that intangible element of “caring” so necessary to nurture life gets lost.

The summer is the best time for
learning and growing. The Jack-
sonville Jewish Center and the
generosity of two families have
made it possible for many young:
sters to attend Camp Ramah fora
totally Jewish experience in a
very relaxed and enjoyable envi-
ronment of a summer camp.
Donations made through Sister-
hood and the Center to the Myra
Thier Lazarus Fund during the
year are also used for this
purpose.

 

 

Center youth that attended Ramah are; Back Row; Jeffrey Harris,
Jeffrey Presser, Joy Pollock, Laura Abramson, Erica Jacobson, Janet
Jacobson. Front Row; Simeon Abramson, Judith Abramson, Sabrina
Katz, Stephanie Katz and Jodi Roey. Not pictured, Nathan Gendzier and
Amy Esman who are serving on the staff of Ramah. Also pictured is
Horty Gendzier, chairwoman of the Ramah Committee, who has devot-
ed many years to see that all children have the opportunity to attend
Ramah.

award to deserving boys and girls. Alan Gordon, son of the late Robert
Gordon, is pictured presenting Ramah Scholarships to Jodi Roey and

Jeffrey Harris.

a perpetual trust for specific
Jewish educational purposes. His
children Iris and Franklin, who

the proceeds of this trust have
also been used to send campers to
Ramah. Joseph Mizrahi present-

“A PROCLAMATION OF JEWISH COMMITTMENT TO ZIONISM”

When in the course of human events is becomes necessary for the Jewish People to
involve themselves with each other and to assume, among the peoples of the earth their
rightful place which the laws of nature and of natures God entitle them, a decent respect
for themselves must be evinced and requires they should act with self interest, declare the
cause which will save them to be their cause and loudly and proudly proclaim themselves

to be Zionists.

We hold these truths to be self evident, to shirk from this proclamation now 1s to
beckon the wolf to the door and into the house, to forget history 1s to relive tt, and for the
support of this proclamation with the love of God we mutually pledge to each other our

lives, our fortunes and our sacred honor.

A. Taffet

Qe

Founded in 1897, the Zionist Organization of America has been a moving spirit toward
the establishment and maintenance of a free, secure, and independent STATE OF
ISRAEL. To be Zionist is to be committed to Israel and the future of the Jewish people.

The Jacksonville district of ZOA has held meetings all year to coordinate committee
activities involving Programs, Public Relations, Membership, and Response to Media.

On April 2, 1978 ZOA sponsored a gala banquet for the Jewish National Fund to honor
the memory of Fran Wilensky. This was the most successful JNF banquet in recent
years.

The President attended the 80th National Convention in Israel and the National
Executive Council in Washington, D.C. and reported to the membership.

An energetic and successful campaign was waged to elect a ZOA slate at the 29th
World Zionist Congress in Jerusalem, attended by Jack Becker.

The objective of ZOA is to interpret and improve the Israeli and Jewish image before
the bar of American public opinion and articulate the Zionist position for our elected
officials to foster American support for Israel and counter the Arab propaganda
campaign.

Commit yourself to the preservation of Jewish identity and dignity everywhere by
becoming a member of ZOA. Any ZOA officer will provide you with membership,
information; or call Irvin Rothstein at 396-3898.

ZIONIST ORGANIZATION OF AMERICA
JACKSONVILLE DISTRICT

P.O. BOX 23780
JACKSONVILLE, FLORIDA
32217

Solomon Schechter Day School P.T.A.

Solomon Schechter P.T.A.

relationships between the parents and teachers. Through its programming,

Jacksonville Jewish Center
Receives Award

 

Rabbi David Gaffney is pictured receiving the Solomon Schechter Award for Worship and Ritual on behalf of

the Jacksonville Jewish Center. Pictured with the Rabbi, left to right; Rabbi Benjamin Kreitman, Executive
Vice-President of the United Synagogue of America; Mrs. Florence Lewis, Chairperson of the Solomon

SOLOMON SCHECHTER AWARD

The Jacksonville Jewish Center was the proud recipient of this
Award for Worship and Ritual Program. The award, the most
distinguished award presented to an individual congregation,
was presented to the Center for excellence and high standards
achieved in the area of worship and ritual.

In Gatlingburg, Tennessee at the Biennial Convention of United
Synagogues, Rabbi Gaffney presented an outline describing the
creative and significant ways in which the beliefs, values and the
traditions of the Jewish people were highlighted through innova-
tive worship and ritual programs at the Jacksonville Jewish
Center.

These included High Holiday Prayerbook, coordination of
Sabbath services and school activities; and the involvement of
lay leaders. The programs of the Sisterhood, Minyanaire, and
Ha’Rayim and all other affiliates of the Center have also con-
tributed to the Jewish education of the congregation.

===== PAGE 69 =====
Jacksonville Hevrah Kaddisha

 

JOEL BRESSLER

President

Communal fraternities for the purpose of providing ~
burial and its attendant rites for the dead have existed
among the Jews ever since the Talmudic period. The
Hebrew term for sucha fraternity is Hevrah Kaddisha (Holy
Society). ae

As soon as the society is notified by the local funeral
home of a Jewish death, out of respect for the dead, a ~
Shomer or watchman is notified and sits with the body,
especially at night, until the time of the funeral.

The practices of cremation, speeding up the
decomposition of the body, or embalming, the slowing
down the process, flowers at the funeral or public viewing of
the body are all discouraged by Jewish custom both
universally and locally.

The Jacksonville Hevrah Kaddish was formed in
January, 1974, and has been functioning ever since. It
consists of about twenty men and women who perform the
necessary tasks on a rotating basis. Anyone who would be
interested in becoming a member of the local society may
contact Joel Bressler, its President, for further information.

History and Activities of Hevrah Kaddisha

The members of the burial society used to attend at the
bedside of the dying person and receive his final declaration
or confession of faith (Viddui). They would wash and dress
the corpse, conduct the burial service, and finally, together
with the observance and performance of the traditional
rites, conduct the actual burial itself. In the case of the poor,
the burial society performed its services without any
charge. In all cases, however, whether there is a charge or
not, the members of the society fulfill their task as a sacred
duty or obligation (Mitzvah) which confers merit upon all
who take part therein. Since respect and acts of charity
shown to the dead were regarded by the Jews in all ages as
the highest form of charity and human service, the
members of the Hevrah Kaddisha have always been highly
respected in the Jewish communities; in many cases in the
past they were accorded special social and religious
privileges in return for their important and unselfish service.

The actual preparation of the dead for burial,
undertaken by the Hevrah Kaddisha, is known in Jewish
law as “taharah” (cleansing, purifying). During this Taharah
definitely prescribed ceremonies are observed,

accompanied by the reading of specific prayers. The body is
washed from head to foot in lukewarm water, dried and
then clad in the burial clothes (Tachrichin). During all this
ceremony the members of the society preserve a proper
gravity and respect. To prevent funerals from becoming
very expensive burdens upon the survivors Rabbon
Gamaiiel 1, in the latter part of the First Century, ordered
that he be buried in a simple linen garment, and this
procedure was soon adopted by the great masses of the
Jewish people. Men are in addition wrapped in the same
Tallit (prayer shawl) which they have worn at services
during their life but with one of the fringes removed. As soon
as the body is fully clothed in the Tachrichin, it is placedina
simple wooden coffin, again in an effort to provide
uniformity in the treatment of the dead and to prevent
excessive cost of burial.

If one wishes to honor their dead by purchasing a more
expensive casket, they are encouraged instead to give
the additional money to the deceased’s favorite loved one’s
charity.

Vreastirer. 3 ee Norman Moss

 

Sam Friedlander, President

Founded 61 years ago as the United Jewish Charities to serve the needy Jews of Jacksonville, and to send money to worthy
Jewish Charities nationally, the Jewish Family & Children’s Services, Inc. became the social work agency of the Jewish
Community. Serving rich and poor, old time families and newcomers, it has, since its professionalization in 1948, served more
than 7,091 families and individuals, more than the number of Jews now estimated to live in Jacksonullle.

The great majority of these persons were served by Lawrence Rackow, the Executive Director who served 25 of these 30
years. He is very ably assisted by Iris Goodstein who proved her competency with all ages, in single or group sessions. In 1977,
490 persons were served in one or more of the four basic programs of the Agency, Counseling, Senior Citizens, Emergency
Assistance and New American programs.

Family problems, especially with children and aging parents and mental health problems exist on all levels. Some families
are beset with many problems and are helped with two or more of our programs.

In the past year, to serve our clients better we added five new groups to our traditional counseling and helping services. The
Friday Afternoon Friendship Group, led by our Director, was started throught the cooperation of River Garden and the
Walter Slaff Memorial Fund. The isolated and boarding home residents completed a year of weekly Sabbath services, meals,
parties and discussions. The “Over 40 Singles Rap Group” led by Allan Kaufman and Iris Goodstein, completed its pilot 10
weeks series, was highly successful and will continue under Mrs. Goodstein. “The Men’s Only Club” at River Garden, led
weekly by our President, Sam Friedlander, is completing its first year and is highly successful. “Tea and Talk” at River Garden
is led by Iris Goodstein and the Mount Carmel Weekly Discussion Group is six months old and is led by Jean Seckeler.

So far three new Russian families were resettled compared with one the previous year. Word is that U.S.S.R. is letting many,
many more out. Will they continue to do so? Will we in Jacksonville do our share? Many volunteers, doctors, dentists, lawyers,
retired persons all are helping. Can you help us? Can we help you? .... Call 396-2941. |

===== PAGE 74 =====
Our Executive Director

Dear Ely:

When I was approached by the Jacksonville Jewish Center to become
its Executive Director, there were a few thoughts across my mind. Being
a New Yorker, the first question was “Where is Jacksonville?” To me,
all of Florida was pretty much the same. Then I asked, “What is the
Jewish Center?” Well this question is still being answered.

I found out that Jacksonville is a working industrial northeast Florida
city unlike Miami as it could be. It is a city with a growth outlook and
cultural opportunities that far outstrip its population. Well, throw one idea
of Florida out the window. It is like one borough of the City of New York,
but smaller than Brooklyn. It has an enviroment favorable to raising
Joshua and supplying my whole family a place to live.

The Jewish community in Jacksonville astounds me. Everyone who
wishes to can have a chance to participate in the Jewish Experience. It }
was refreshing to find such involvement in the community on every level, A
youth, young marrieds, middle life people, Seniors. Everyone here Jonathan E Koll
seems to know that their survival as a people is intrinsically linked to their |
living as a community. There has been great contribution to this city both philanthropically and economically by the Jewish
community, I found.

As of yet, we have not explored all the surrounding territories. I understand that you could end up a perpetual tourist in the
southeast as we are located near all of the states in the area. We do hope to get to those places, too.

What is the Jewish Center? This one could fill this note to a point ad infinitum. There are a lot of people here. I came at the
end of an outgoing administration that had succeeded in bringing into line a tenuous financial situation. The incoming adminis-
tration is one of excitement and involvement. They believe, as I, that a synagogue is a place for people, a Bet Am. They call
for involvement of everyone in all phases of the work.

There are committees for everything . . . . a Finance Committee, Membership, House, Ritual, Youth, Adult Activities,
Education Committees. Interestingly enough all are well peopled. They do have a lot of work here. The re-vitalization of the
Center will depend on how hard they work. The Educaton and Youth Committees have ajump on the others with the coming of
Dov Kentof. He is the Director of these-programs and could, if we harness his energy, supply the electricity for the:place for at
least six months. The Membership Committee has expanded its operation, not only to bring people to the Center, but help
all of the community with its hospitality approach.

The facilities are beautiful. With the building being only 3 years old, all of the potentials have not been realized. Now, we are in
the process of furnishing the interior of the Sanctuary so our worship experience can be even more complete. The pool, gym and
tennis courts are nice to have around, believe me.

I work with a great staff. Florence Robin and Esther Fishler are the secretaries, and being new here, I could be lost without
them. Mr. Kenneth Bowden and Inez Achord are my right hands in the financial end. Blanche Slott is a woman with a coat of
many colors, if it is winter, she is coordinating activities, caterers and Bar and Bat Mitzvahs; if it is summer, she is running the
Day Camp.

They have a full time Day School for kids from age 3 to sixth grade. Josh is going there this year. When he gets older, I under-
stand that academically it’s a great place to be.

Well, that’s about all for now. Ely, Jacksonville is a nice place to be. The living is slower, the climate abit nicer, the apartment
a lot bigger and the people are friendly. The Jewish Center is a great place at which to work.

I feel that this year will be a banner year for us all.

Regards from Shirley and Josh for a Healthy, Safe New Year,

Regards,
Jon

A 25th Anniversary is a “once in a life-time” milestone in anyone’s life. The Mr. and Mrs. club, an affiliate of the Jacksonville Jewish Center,
will reach that pinnacle in November 1978 and will celebrate at a special Silver Jubilee.

Boasting of the longest continuous existence of any social club in the Jewish community, the membership of the Mr. and Mrs. Club consists
of just what its name implies. It was organized a quarter of a century ago for the sole purpose of promoting fellowship, friendships and good-
times for married Jewish couples.

Dances are held every month to the music of live bands. Meals and snacks are served and each committee decides on its own type of enter- »
tainment. All of this is climaxed with a gala New year’s dance including a cocktail party, full-course dinner and professional entertainment.

As this organization is non-profit, all of the aforementioned is enjoyed at a nominal cost. There is no age limit and membership is open to the
entire married Jewish community.

Top Row; Commander Sidney Engelhardt, Cubmaster, .Henry Ohayon, Stuart Haft, Robert Kantor, Bruce Gelman, Scott Panken.
and Andrew Kulchin. Middle Row; Randy Graff, Eric Greenman, Michael Biller, Kenney Haft, Bobby Greenspoon, Jeffrey Schweb,

John De Paul, Scott Rosin. Bottom Row; Lon Gilbert, Joel Neid, Jeff Wolfson, Andrew Jaffa, Randy Finder, Danny Miller, Danny
Simon, David Garber and Adam Jacobson.

Cub Scouting as an introduction to Boy Scouts, plays an outstanding role for boys between the ages of 8 and 10. The boys advance
from Wolf to Bear and then on to Webelos.

In this day and age when there are so many temptations, our boys find themselves interested in completeing their achievements
toward a higher rank, and participating in the monthly pack meeting. There they put on skits and show off their crafts.

During the year, they shared in many new and different experiences. They look forward to the Annual Pinewood and Rocket
Derbys, Scout Sabbath and the Blue and Gold Banquet.

To close the year, members of Pack 14 and their families gathered at the Center Day Campsite for an afternoon of swimming and
cooking over an open fire. The highlight of the event was the presentation of the Larry Hacker Cub Scout Award. It went to the
Cub with the best attendance at Den and Pack Meetings, achievements and good will. The receipient for 1978 was Stuart Haft;
an individual plaque was presented to Stuart and his name has been added to the permanent plaque which remains at the Center.

Nostalgia Night
At Che Center

10101 San Jose Poulevard

 
 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

Over 400 people attended a congre-
gational celebration held at the Center
to toast the sale of Third and Silver.
Home movies and a special slide pre-
sentation, including commentary on
each were throughly enjoyed by all.
Fifty years of memorabilia of Third
and Silver were on display. The evening
included the installation of Officers
and the Board of Directors.

Top Row; Scoutmaster Don Kupfer, Ian
Mann, Don Levinson, Irving Sucholechi,
Josh Weiss, Gary Kupfer. Bottom Row;
Mark Levinson, Ronnie Mizrahi, Roy
Sucholechi, Sam Kersun, Alan Biller,

Gary Kupfer receiving Eagle rank from
Pete House - Shawnee District Leader-
ship Trainer.

i

 
 

Scout Master Don Kupfer, Eagle Scout
Gary Kupfer.

 

Troop 14

 

The year of 1977-1978 has seen Troop 14 make a transition from a group of boys just
attending a meeting, toa group of young men getting together for a purpose. Good fellow-
ship, learning to turn energy into purposeful action and earning merit badges for
advancement, had their place at all meetings and outings.

Camping trips were also a integral part of troop activities and all scouts have become
good campers and familiar with the out-of-doors.

We are proud that Gary Kupfer attained the rank of Eagle Scout which will blaze
the trail for many more Eagles for Troop No. 14, as wellas Life rank which was attained
by Josh Weiss.

Ian Mann and Irving and Roy Sucholeiki obtained much scout know-how at the
summer session of the North Florida Scout Camp. Josh Weiss and Gary Kupfer shared
a unique experience by having the opportunity to attend the scout encampment at
Philmont, New Mexico. The trip was filled with adventures and new activities from
which the entire troop will benefit.

During the coming year we will strive to maintain our momentum and continue
to grow. We welcome the new scouts who have joined our ranks from the Webelos
Cub Scouts and look forward to sharing all scout activities with them. There will be new
experiences and continuing adventures for the not so new scouts.

With the help of my two assistant scoutmasters, Harry Berman and Nathan Weiss,
we will continue to do our part to keep Troop No. 14 both enjoyable and rewarding for
all scouts.

Yours for Better Scouting,
Donald Kupfer, Scoutmaster

 

Pack Inspection - Everette Thompson
Shawnee District Executive

Cantor’s 30th Concert

Featured:

Cantor David Lefkowitz ..................... Guest
Soloist ............... Augmented with Terry Parker
Soloist 233 a Waune Balley

Chorus

The Proud Marton Family, L-R; Bill Rubenstein, Barbara, Pearl
Cantor Marton, Shelley Bobb and her husband Manny.

} Bo i a is ee h eee $ 2

The Choral Society conducted by Ronald Zapen and Wayne Bailey, augmented with Terry Parker Chorus,
delighted the quests with musical selections from the Sacred Service byErnest Bloch, PartI andII. Andamost
moving “Eli Yivneh Haagilil” and concluded with Halleluyah.

I wish to take this opportunity to thank the congregation and to express my appreciation to the Leadership for the testimonial dinner in honor
of my thirty-one years as Cantor of the Jacksonville Jewish Center. Coming to Jacksonville in September 1946, to a comparatively smaller con-
gregation than exists now, and finding the position open to all facets of synagogue life, I found myself serving in all capacities for eight months. ~

The congregation at that time needed a volunteer chorus to enhance the religious services. Consequently, the Center Choral Society was organized
consisting of a group of dedicated men and women who presented concerts of Jewish music annually and enhanced the Friday night services.
They also provided some of the finest liturgical music for the High Holy Days. This choral group went on to present thirty consecutive concerts —
dedicated to the Cantor’s Assembly, and for that I am very grateful to each and every member of the Choral Society.

In the religious school the children’s choir, namely the Center Glee Club, often appeared with the Choral Society and their particular contribution
was Israeli, Chasidic and Yiddish music. At times this group consisted of as many as 70 children.

In 1947 it was necessary to organize a Talit and Tefflin Club for the sole purpose of teaching the boys Bar Mitzvah skills and prayer. This club grew
to a membership of between 75 and 80 boys and for 26 years its success was a source of pride to me. In those years it was necessary for a Cantor
to also serve as an educator and it was my privilege to serve as a full staff member of the Hebrew school for 27 years.

The most meaningful contribution I made was the preparation of approximately 1400 Bar and Bat Mitzvahs. Our close association led to lasting
friendships. I enjoyed officiating at the many weddings uniting loved ones and shared the deep sense of loss in times of sorrow. Of all the services
performed I felt the visitations of the sick of the Jewish community was most deeply appreciated.

Pearl and I derived great pleasure seeing our daughters Barbara and Shelley married at the Jacksonville Jewish Center where the children had grown
up and celebrated their Bat Mitzvahs.

Jewish history and Jewish problems created a sense of urgency
towards the State of Israel, therefore, I dedicated my life to the cause
of “Zionism”. Having served seven years as President of the local
Z. O. A., I feel the need to instill in the younger generation a sense
of responsibility to continue to serve the State of Israel and the
Jewish people.

These 32 years have been very challenging and at the same time
greatly rewarding.

Pearl and I wish everyone a most happy and healthy New Year.
SHALOM AL YISRAEL
Abraham Marton

 

 
 

Cantor David Lefkowitz, guest soloist, augmented with Terry 21
Parker Chorus and soloist Wayne Bailey.

===== PAGE 102 =====
ORT of Jacksonville

ORT, the Organization for Rehabilitation through Training, is an
international organization which for almost a century has brought
freedom to world Jewry. ORT operates in 30 countries with 76,500
students learning 90 trades in 800 training units. They represent
people gaining an education and a future career in architectural
drawing, telecommunications, avionics, hotel management, machine
design, computer technology and more. Special programs are avail-
able for the physically handicapped, the aged, and language training
programs for immigrants bound for English speaking countries.
Different courses for different people with different needs and qualifi-
cations are offered.

Jacksonville Area Council helps support the ORT program and
consists of five Chapters each meeting at different times to serve the
needs of all the women in the community. The Area Council is the body
which services the five ORT chapters.

The following are the officers of the Area Council and the Five
Chapters and their meeting days:

 

ORT AREA COUNCIL — Seated (left to right): Dinah Kossoff, Recording Secretary; Sylvia Davidson, Parliamentarian; Paula Klausner,
President; Anita Warschoff, Vice-President (Membership); Madelyn Glossman, Vice-President (Education). Standing (left to right): Joanne
LeDoyen, Treasurer; Sue Ann Blumenthal, Corresponding Secretary; Karen Backilman, Financial Secretary; Judy Greenhut, Vice-President
(Special Projects). Missing from picture: Terry Rosin.

===== PAGE 114 =====
U.S.Y.’ers Terri Katz, Jay Plotkin, Susan DuBow, Lawrence Ansbacher kibbitzing with Gene Kornblum.

Well, here we are again looking back on another great year of Jax. U.S.Y. Thanks to an able-bodied board and the very
capable leadership of our loving parents, alias aavisors Bob and Barbara Cantor, we are able to say that 1977-1978 was
another successful campaign for the §5§ members of our group.

This year was one of the most successful in recent times in the area of 9th graders, the youngest members of our group.
We had some 20 active 9th graders who proved a great asset to the group. A large reason for this is due to a program held
last summer. The program, a cookout and swim party at the Day Camp was designed for 9th graders, prospective
members, and a few borderline members. The new board along with some of our stronger members spent the day talking
to the new kids and explaining what U.S.Y. is all about. The 3§ who attended went away with some new feelings about
U.S.Y. Later in the summer we also had our traditional trip down Itchatucknee Springs. Once we got the inner tubes on
the bus, we all managed to have a good time.

The regular year started off with a bang with a ‘Get To Know Each Other And Make Your Own Sundae Party” and an
“Anything Goes Night’’. Both were under the very capable leadership of Programming Vice-President, Terri Katz, and
averaged some 45 people per event. As the year moved on we all began to get ready for the fall Sub-Regional Convention
which was held in Jacksonville on November 11-13. U.S.Y.’ers from Orlando to Sarasota came expecting atypically great
Jacksonville Convention - and they weren’t disappointed. Chairmen Lawrence Ansbacher and Nathan Gendzier worked
extremely hard to create a weekend that was worthy of an International Chapter Recognition Award.

Throughout this past year the cry has been heard all over at conventions around the state that Jax. U.S.Y. is moving on
and onward. We continued from convention to convention from Hayrides to Square Dances and from Skating Parties to
Walk-a-thons. This year we had several achievements from our members, and our club, that the whole congregation can
be proud. We had a Regional Officer, a Sub-Regional Officer, several members of the Regional Projects Board and also
sent five delegates to the International Convention in Toronto. We also won an award for our large contribution to the
Tikun Olam (a U.S.Y. based charity). Things are looking to continue along the same lines next year as we already have
another Regional Officer elected and several members of the Projects Board. Also two of the three Regional Conventions
will be hosted by members of Jax. U.S.Y. We are on the way to contributing even more to Tikun Olam next year.

As you can see, it was a very productive year for the members of J.U.S.Y. and things are taking shape for next year. In
conclusion, I just want to say that I hope our new officers elected in September can be as proud as we are of this year. It
has been a year where great strides in religious and social action programs have continued to take place. Pregrams such
as our River Garden Shabbats and our U.S.Y. services are all very inspiring. Overall it has been a year to remember and

those that were a part of it will not soon forget it. Jay Plotkin

Officers 1978-79

President ........ Rae: Sandra Rosenberg
1st Vice-President ............. Mona Schaffer
2nd Vice-President .............6- Juli Shapiro
MiMOm roo ee ... Jeanne Maron
Secretary ...... weeeceeeeeeese Melinda Zisser
Treasurer... oes. 60ss sees Michelle Sussman
PHSIONIGN es oo oe ... SuziLissner
Parliamentarian .......... Michelle Honigman

In the past year, BBG has done everything. Surprise breakfasts, creative services, Sweetheart Heart
Throb Weekend,a Sukkah building party are just a few of the events. We are very proud of our annual
NO-TALKATHON which raised $250.00 for Hope Haven Children’s Hospital. Visiting the Children’s
Ward with our own Raggedy Anne was a very pleasant surprise for the children. There also was tubing
down the Itchetucknee. Great times are always had at conventions and training sessions.

Plans have already been made for the coming year. Many of our successful functions will be re-
peated, by popular demand of course. We have begun attending Friday Evening Services on a rotating
basis between all the synagogues of Jacksonville. A trip to Disneyworld and tubing are in the offing.

It is difficult to contact all the Jewish girls of Jacksonville, so if you are interested and are in grades;
eighth through twelfth, please call Sandra Rosenberg 731-5543 or Jeanne Maron 733-0966. We would

love to have you join us.

BRU GIRLS

WHO DEPRIVES US OF OUR RIGHTS?

We have the right to speak our mind, the right to have a family, the right to work,
the right to a good life, the right to worship as we see fit.

We all realize how much these and other of our deepest
rights are threatened today. We’re ready to struggle
to protect our rights from being taken from us by others.

Yet we don't always see that the security of our rights
can be taken from us . . . by ourselves.

There is no way, for example, to enjoy our right to a
family without fulfilling our duty to care for our children.

It is no different with other rights. All experience and
history makes clear that no right stands alone.

We can enjoy a right only by accepting the duty
which is the other side.

THE RIGHT TO SPEAK OUR MIND

is inseparable from our duty to listen; to give those
around us the same rights of expression we want for
ourselves.

THE RIGHT TO HAVE A FAMILY

is made meaningless if we postpone spending time with
them. It is a familiar delusion to think that our greatest
duty is to provide those we love with material security
alone. They may need our presence and active love
even more.

THE RIGHT TO WORK

to earn our livelihood in ways consistent with our
talent is taken for granted. But we fail our duty when we
do not give our work a full measure of integrity and
effort.

THE RIGHT TO A GOOD LIFE

cannot be “given” to us. We are given only the oppor-
tunity. There still remains our duty and action to achieve
it. The ability to reflect on the precious gift of time;

to give of ourselves, not only of our means; to sense that
we are never so old that we cannot learn is fundamental
to a good life.

THE RIGHT TO WORSHIP GOD

is so important when we see how much evil in human
affairs comes from the tendency to mistake a part
of life - - power, status, possession, for the whole of it.

Even in our beliefs, the right to worship God is also
the duty not to fall into the idolatry of worshipping
any part of this creation as though it were the whole.

IT IS CLEAR, THEN, THAT TO ENJOY

OUR RIGHTS WE MUST FULFILL

OUR DUTIES.

Our individual rights are insured only when we achieve
a society where all are concerned with their duties
and only then.

East Coast Fruit Co.

LOUIS PORTNOY —

WALTER PORTNOY

AND FAMILIES

Sunday School of
Jacksonville Jewish Center

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

The Sunday School of the Jacksonville Jewish Center is planned
expressly for children in Kindergarten, first and second grades. A two
hour program gives each child the opportunity to experience the
richness of their Jewish Heritage through song, dance, role playing,
cooking, stories, games and arts and crafts. Emphasis is placed on
the holidays and festivals. Jewish values and mitzvot are encouraged.

Samuel Thier

It hath been told thee Oman, what is good, and what
The Lord doth require of Thee:
Only to do justly and to love mercy,
And to walk humbly with thy God.
Micah 6:8

This verse describes Papa so well, that it seems almost
unnecessary to say more. This was Papa’s favorite verse -
one which he made sure to teach us all. He did more than just
teach us the words though. He provided us a living example.
No man has ever adhered so completely to the principle of
loving and having faith in his fellow man. As such, his deeds
were all ones of justice, mercy and love.

Papa’s strength as a teacher, a friend, as Father, Grand-
father or as a family role was his attitude towards his own
actions. They came so naturally, with such love and humility.
He saw the best in all situations and brought it out with his
own special humor. A lesson taught this way - - with love and
dignity yet made fun by humor - - can never be forgotten.

 

Edith Gendzier
Granddaughter of
Sam Thier

It is with a great sense of loss that the members of the Jacksonville Jewish Center pay tribute to Sam Thier.
Sam served the Center in so many ways. He was a member and a Chairman of many committees including
Membership, Religious, Education, Executive and Cemetery. His personal devotion and commitment to Jewish
values was demonstrated by his participation in daily minyan and Shabbat and holiday services. He was especially
devoted to the men and women at River Garden Home for the Aged. Rabbi Gaffney referred to Sam as the
“Davaner Extraordinaire”. And rightly so. His traditional “davening” at Minha Services on Yom Kippur at the

Center was always an inspiration for the entire congregation. A beautiful Jewish legend has it that the world exists
by reason of 36 righteous people who by their tenderness, modesty and dignity the community is sustained. Sam
Thier was a rare person who succeeded in creating an atmosphere and aura with the ability to inspire others by a
sense of presence. This year there will be an emptiness at High Holiday Services because of his absence. But his
memory lingers . . . his family carries on, with the same love and devotion to the synagogue that he loved. We are
all better people because we knew Sam.

The Progressive Credit Union was established, and is operated to serve the financial needs of its members consisting of men
and women of the Jacksonville Jewish Community.

In 1977, we assisted many Jacksonville businessmen and families with loans for automobiles, appliances, marriages, and other
worthwhile personal needs. Under a special program designed to aid students with College tuition, 32 Jewish students were
assisted in their higher education. The Student Loan Program is fully guaranteed by the U. S. Government.

All members receive free Life Insurance on their savings, up to $2,000.00. All loans are covered with free Credit Life Insurance
for the full outstanding balances. With all of our extra services rendered, the ratio charged for interest is lower than that of other
lending institutions in the area. All Savings are insured up to $40,000.00 by the Florida Credit Union Guarantee Company. |

We extend a cordial invitation for all to join with us in the Progressive Credit Union to build a stronger Jewish Community
in Jacksonville. For further information, please call Mr. Joe Cohen at 354-4770.

 

Organized in 1960, Cassia Garden Circle, an affiliate of the Garden Club of Jacksonville, is a
group of Jewish women who are interested in gardening. Many of the charter members are still
active in the group.

Cassia is a group of tropical trees, shrubs and plants with compound leaves, yellow flowers and
long pods, found in Israel. The Garden Club meets monthly. Programs have included Japanese
Flower Arranging, bonsai, hanging baskets, underwater arrangements and potpourri. Many
exciting plans have been made for next year’s programming. Spend an interesting morning, and
enjoy a light lunch with some pleasant ladies. If you are interested call Rhoda Goldstein 733-7275.

Mt. Carmel Gardens

President... Israel Schemer
lst Vice-President ..... beeen coe. Kitty Flanselman
2nd Vice-President ................ .. Jennie Riolino
3rd Vice-President -..3.....5.. 2... .... AlGottlieb
reassure... 0.3. 6: eu oe oe rene Enrich
Secretary. ee a. Pole. Ethel Kor
Mount Carmel Gardens is a Senior Citizens home, seventeen floors high, Corresponding Secretary .......... Pauline Schemer

and 208 apartments. It was originally planned for Jewish tenants, but now
houses about thirty percent Gentiles, all living together as congenial neighbors -
almost like one big family.

Every apartment on each floor is “door” checked every morning by the “floor
captain”, to make sure each individual tenant is okay. Each apartment has an
emergency cord, which can be pulled if a tenant is physically in need of help, this
brings someone to their apartment almost immediately, - - and the rescue
squad is at their door within minutes if necessary. There is a fire and smoke
alarm system throughout the building, which, thank goodness, has only been
used for drills; - the office staff is kindly, courteous and anxious to serve; - and at
night the outside doors are locked, and there is a Security Guard on duty, who
makes hourly checks to be sure all is well.

The tenants have formed a “Mount Carmel Tenants Association’, which has
monthly meetings to discuss the business and problems which may arise. The
“Association” has installed a stereo and sound system, an outdoor barbeque
grill, and a small platform-stage, for the tenants use.

Regular educational activities are available for anyone who cares to attend,
including: once a week - discussion group; - choral group; - health education
and weight control; - oil painting; - creative needle craft; - and ceramics; - twicea
month, book review; - and retirement education.

As for social gatherings, there is a birthday party once a month honoring all
the tenants who have birthdays during that month. These always include
entertainment from volunteer “outsiders”, and refreshments furnished by the
birthday celebrants - and end with dancing to Fannie Kaufman’s piano playing
or to stereo music. Every Saturday night there is a dance held in the auditorium.
Twice a week Bingo games are enjoyed; - and once a month an “extra-party-of-
the-month” is held - each “extra party” is something different. There are cook-
outs and covered dish luncheons. And of course there are trips - to Jekyll
Island, Disney World, White Springs, Jai Alai at Daytona; to Theater
Jacksonville productions, symphonettes, ballets, Broadway South Dinner
Theater, Alhambra Dinner Theater and many others. Once a year there is a
combined Christmas-Chanakah party, complete with menorahs, Christmas
tree, dreidles, colored lights - all the symbols of both holidays - a Santa Claus,
and gifts for all. Of course, there is a New Year’s Eve dinner and dance.

Once a month a Newsletter is published of building and tenant news, and
distributed free to all the tenants. There are two volunteer entertainment
groups: The Carmel Comics, under the leadership of Celia Falis, who have
performed extensively wherever they are invited; also, The Carmel Singers,
under the direction of Mary Lou Weldy, entertain throughout the city; - and all
members of both groups receive R.S.V.P. (Retired Senior Volunteer
Programs) credit. Fannie Kaufman, our piano player, is blind, - but can and will
play anytime, anything and anywhere - whenever she is asked.

This is Mount Carmel Gardens - a high-rise building complex, filled with
contented and friendly Senior Citizens.

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

Submitted by:
Pauline Schemer

JEWIS
EDUCATIO

THERE’S NO SAYING WHEN

Message by Rabbi Dov Kentof
Educational and Youth Director

 
 

What constitutes the minimum in Jewish Education?

My answer is: There is no minimum in Jewish Education.

Education in its true sense is not the same as learning a skill. Education is not a product or an acquisition.
you cannot acquire the skill to type, to fix a leak, to ride a bicycle.

Education is not merely teaching or learning. No one can sell you, or give you, or deliver you an education, as
one can a book or a picture. you can acquire the ability to type, to fix aleak, or to ride a bicycle, but you cannot
acquire an education. Someone can teach you, and you can learn any number of abilities or attitudes or appre-
ciations, and you may still not have an education.

What, then, is education? It is a continuous, steady progression in which the acquiring of one ability, attitude,
or appreciation leads directly to another, and that in turn to still another, andso on. The test of an education lies
not in the particular content you acquire, but in the need or desire which you feel to add to that content.

Long ago our Rabbis said, “udlo mosif yoseif’ — “He who does not add to his learning, decreases it.”” Years
later, John Dewey, America’s greates philosopher, expressed the same thought when he said, “The most
important attitude that can be formed (in education) is the desire to go on learning.”

Like any education, Jewish education involves certain skills that may be mastered, certain information that
may be acquired, certain attitudes that may be developed. But, also like any education, Jewish education in the
true sense of the term, must be conceived as an on-going process of inspiring students, young and old, with
both the desire and the ability to keep on learning.

Viewed from this angle, the quality of learning is more important than the quantity. In other words, more
important than “How mucthas been learned?” is the question
“How well has learning taken place?”

Quality in education is determined by the learner’s desire
to learn more. And an appetite for learning cannot be acquired
on a diet of teaspoonful doses. A certain amount of quantity
is necessary...

Therefore, Jewish education cannot be ended at any stage
in life. There is no number of years or Jewish study which we
can call enough. There is no list of accomplishments which
separates in itself the educated from the uneducated Jew.
There is no package of facts, no collection of textbooks, no
battery of tests, the mastery or the possession of which justi-
fies the boast of possessing a Jewish education.

The good Jewish teacher will so teach that his pupils want to
go on and on in their Jewish studies. The good Jewish school
encourages and facilitates this desire, and the good Jewish
parent backs up the teacher and the school to his utmost in
word and deed.

On the building of the university there is an inscription which
reads: “Education is not amere means of life. Education is life.”

And that is why you cannot “say when” to Jewish Education.

IN SUPPORT OF SCOUTING

For many years the Center Sister-
hood has actively sponsored the Brownie
and Girl Scout troops. Scouting is a
very important phase of the youth
program in the Center. The Scouting
program helps girls to develop charac-
ter, to be useful at home, to love nature
and the out-of-doors, to create objects
of beauty and usefulness from simple
things, and to find their place in Synago-
que and community service.

Top Row, L-R; Alyse Slaff, Allison Warren, Karen Manasse, Orit Katz. Front Row, L-R;
Elaine Cantor, Stacy Ginsburg, Stacy Cohen, Beth Greenburg.

The Brownie Troop No. 147 was led by ClaraMannandshewasassisted byIdlene
Slaff. The Brownies began the year with an Investiture for the new Brownies
and had a play which was followed by refreshments.

During the year they did many arts and crafts and went on nature walks. Some
days they just played games and had fun with each other. They made different types
of things. The year ended with a cook-out at the day camp grounds. There was
swimming also.

A ceremony was held for the Brownies who were moving up to Girl Scouts.

Written by

Alyse Slaff

Religious Services — Traditional prayer services are held
daily, morning and evening in the Witten Chapel. Friday
Family Services, Sabbath and Holiday Services are observed
throughout the entire year.

Personal Religious Observances — Traditional Jewish
customs are observed in connection with Circumcision,
Naming of Girls, Pidyon Ha-ben, Bar Mitzvah, Bat Mitzvah,
Confirmation, Marriages, Conversion to Judaism,
Bereavement.

Education — Daily Kindergarten and Nursery with
outstanding program of secular and religious content.
Hebrew School, carefully graded with intensive Hebrew
content, including Jewish history and customs, Sunday
School for kindergarten, first and second grades: Solomon
Schechter Day School, a graded secular and Hebrew
School, 1st through 6th grades; Hebrew High School with
Pre-Jr. and Junior Congregations and Minyanaire.

Extension Services — Library, outstanding collection of
juvenile and adult Jewish books; Adult Education, religious
and cultural Jewish courses; music programs of religious and
cultural Jewish music; P.T.A.; Sisterhood and Ha’Rayim, a
fellowship of young families; social activities provided by Mr.
and Mrs. Club, Single Adults; Family Recreation Program
(Olympic pool), summer weekdays and Sundays for all ages.
Youth Activities — ATID for college age youth; United
Synagogue Youth, various age levels are served by Center
Youth League, Charles Palot Club, Kadima, Boy Scouts,
Cub Scouts, Girl Scouts, Brownies; Sunday Funday;

Rabbi’s Message

“LET THEM MAKE ME A SANCTUARY SO THAT I SHALL
DWELL IN THEIR MIDST”
(Exodus XXV.8)

The words above were spoken to our people early in their history. They were still
wanderers in a wasteland, but they were taking their first steps onto the stage of
history. Surrounded in the desert by all kinds of foes, not knowing to what dangers
they would be exposed, they were asked to build a Sanctuary and to place this
Sanctuary in the very center of their encampment. The synagogue was to become
the focal point of their life. We, living so many centuries later, in an utterly different
part of the world under radically different conditions still heed this ancient instruction.
What is the place of this synagogue? . . . What is its goal? . .. What function is it re-
quired to fulfill? . . . for our perplexed and confused age.

The first task of the synagoque is to conserve, to guard against oblivion on the great
insights and concepts that we have developed in the past. To some extent every age
is nihilistic. It would like to think that history begins with it. In the midst of a life that
is constantly changing (as it should), and in a world subject to so many changing
fashions, the synagogue proclaims “Do not destroy the ancient landmarks”. The
greatest achievement of the human being is his history, culture and civilization.
No one generation could accomplish this for itself. There is an accumulation of exper-
ience that no one could attain in one brief life span. Life moves forward as it must,
but the basic nature of the human spirit cannot be destroyed. The synagogue con-
serves for the people of Israel its grand bequests of earlier generations that helped
to ennoble this human spirit.

The second task of the synagogue is to scrutinize, to criticize, to evaluate. We are
called upon to make many decisions . . . as individuals, as Jews, as members of a
community. We need a focal point - the synagogue... and the lessons learned therein
to help evaluate life and our decisions in terms of the highest standards. We need an
ethical code, a direction, that sees the problems of our time from the perspective of a
great background of experience and faith. The synagogue helps to refine our goals
so that we can see our individual and collective paths in the context of life’s highest
principles.

The synagoque seeks to enlarge the lives of people. We tend to live in the presence
of the immediate. We know the world of buildings, roads, plants and factories. There
is within the human being however a dimension not fulfilled by these. No only the facts
of life, but the meaning of life is necessary for a well ordered human existence. The
synagogue enlarges our experience by guiding our perspective to include loftier
visions.

Lastly, the synagogue reminds the Jew that what keeps us together is not a
common sorrow, nor the need for a common defense. We have been touched by
sorrow . .. we have responded to the needs of our brothers. But what unites us is a
common history, a common culture, a common faith and a common hope. A syna-
gogue without holiness, without prayer, without study, without the great moments of
Sabbaths and holidays becomes a faint image of what the Sanctuary was to be.

As we link our lives and those of our loved ones with the synagogue, we commit
ourselves to conserve the best within us, to condemn what is unworthy of us and to
share together this great and glorious faith which is part of us.

As we begin our New Year as a congregation with new officers, new leaders and
new Directors, may we never forget that we share together great illumination of our
synagogue’s purpose so that we may have a Jewish community of dignity, decency
and affection.

Inge and I join in wishing all a healthy and fulfilling New Year.

Rabbi David Gaffney

 

Rabbi David Gaffney

 

 

The Gathieu Gilden
Shalva - 18, Asher - 19

MEN’S BASKETBALL

 

Many. men of the Jewish Community look forward to Tuesday evenings when they can play
a competitive game of basketball in the Fletcher-Selber Auditorium of the Jacksonville
Jewish Center.

 

With great precision and ruach the Savion Dance
Group performs for the Jewish community at the
celebration of Israel’s 30th birthday. The dancers
are left to right Ilana Manasse, Brenda Jacobs, Robin
Morris, Sheila Jacobson, Joan Levin, Deborah
Bressler, Carol Perras and Judy Silverman.

 

THE SAVION DANCE GROUP was formed two years ago by Ilana Manasse and Joan Levin after they returned from a Fred Berk Israeli Dance Work-
shop at Camp BlueStar. The troupe has performed for many groups in the community. Recently they represented Israel at the Multi-Ethnic Festival
at the Civic Auditorium for the 4th of July Celebration. The group meets once a week at the Center. For information call Ilana 733-7765.

The Afternoon Hebrew School

The Afternoon Hebrew School of the Jacksonville Jewish Center is designed for students in grades 3 - 7 and provides an
elementary education leading to Bar/Bat Mitzvah and continuation in the high school program.
Meeting twice during the week and on Saturdays, “the basics” are at the core of the curriculum, with stress on Hebrew Reading,
Jewish Social Studies and current events relating to Israel are discussed throughly. Synagogue skills, holiday observances and
knowledge of prayers are all part of the comprehensive afternoon Hebrew School program.

YOUTH DIRECTOR

The Jewish Center has endeavored to provide the most comprehensive active and
qualitative youth program. To attain this goal, two individual youth groups now
meet all the needs of children ranging from 7th grades, Kadima - 7th and 8th
grades, U.S.Y. - 9th-12th grades. All programs are supervised under the direction
of Dov Kentof, Education and Youth Director.

The philosophy of The Jewish Center Youth is to provide an informal setting for
reinforcement of formal classroom training and to attract its members by offer-
ing a well-balanced program of social activities, learning experiences and religious
and cultural exposure. |

Experience has shown us that one of the most effective methods of imparting
Jewish values is to live them. Consequently, we have oriented ourselves toward
providing a substantial number of overnight and week-end retreats whereby
the beauty of Shabbat and the ethical message of Judaism can be more effective,
generating many times the enthusiasm that the confining walls and artificial
setting of the classroom have to offer.

In addition to a year round program of retreats, cultural activities, socials and
athletics, The Jewish Center affiliation with United Synagogue of America makes
it possible for our members to attend a variety of nationally sponsored summer
programs with scholarship funds available. Many of our youth can testify to the
benefits they received while touring AmericanJewish communities (USY on
W heels), attending Camp Ramah, or spending the summer in Israel (USY Israel
Pilgrimage) or Russia (USY Eastern Europe Pilgrimage).

To sum it all up, alot is happening. The opportunities to meet new people in Jewish
surroundings and experience Jewish living in unique and exciting situations
are endless. No matter what your interest, we have something for you.

My wife Alina, Tali, Gil and myself, wish all of you a very Happy New Year.

 

 

 

Alina Kentof

 

Gil Kentof

ISRAEL AWARDS

 

JERUSALEM AWARD

The Jacksonville Jewish Center
in conjunction with State of. Israel Bonds
cordially invites you to a

TESTIMONIAL

for

SAMUEL J. THIER

at which he will be presented the coveted

 
 

SAMUEL THIER

Jerusalem Award

 

representing economic strength and solidarity with Israel
for his dedicated and outstanding service to the Jewish Community

This award was presented to Sam Thier on
October 2, 1977

MAIMONIDES AWARD

 

t

Dr. Samuel G. Rosenthal Dr. Roy Sloat

“LET US STAND SILENT”
in memory of our dearly beloved
sons and daughters who gave their
lives for the liberation of our home-
land and the security of our people.
They gave all they had. They
poured out their very lifeblood for
the freedom of Israel, even as the
living waters quench the thirst of
the arid soil. Not in monuments of
stones or trees shall be preserved
their memory, but in the reverence
and pride which will, until the end of
time, fill the hearts of our people
when their memory is recalled.

“Our hearts are filled to overflow-
ing with praise and thanksgiving
to the Rock of Israel. But let us not
delude ourselves that our work is
finished. We are still at the begin-
ning. The road stretching ahead is
long and hard, and there are still
many obstacles in our way... The
sword is still girded round our loins;
let us not boast as men who have
taken it off.

“On our festive day let us review in
joy and thanksgiving the mighty
deeds of the past and let us resolve
to apply ourselves with all our
might and all our heart to the new
efforts of the future”.

(David Ben Gurion on Israel’s
Independence Day, May 4th, 1949)

Dr. Samuel G. Rosenthal and Dr. Roy Sloat were honored with the Maimonides Award by the State of Israel Bonds at a grand tribute

dinner on Sunday, November 6 at the Jacksonville Jewish Center.

The two men are only the second and third members of the health professions to receive this prestigious award. The first recipient was

Dr. Albert Sabin, who helped develop the polio vaccine.

The award is named for Moses Maimonides, the “Rambam,” outstanding rabbinic authority, codifier of Jewish law, philosopher and

physician. Born in Spain in 1135, he fled to Morocco, then Egypt where he did most of his work.

Dr. Seymour Alpert was the speaker at the dinner. A vice-president of George Washington University, Dr. Alpert is international chairman

for Health and Professions for the State of Israel Bonds.

The Jewish Center is very proud that this award was given to two of its members for their efforts on behalf of Israel and the Jewish

community.

===== PAGE 215 =====
Jacksonville

 

Chapter of Hadassah

Hadassah, the largest Zionist organization in the world, as well
as the largest women’s voluntary organization in the United
States, has a membership of 360,000 members who dedicate
themselves to help Israel, a developing democracy, to help itself.

The Jacksonville Chapter has approximately 900 members
and consists of two groups. Tikva, which meets during the day
and Hannah Senesch which meets in the evening.

(The Shir-Li group has combined with the Tikvah Group)

Hadassah celebrated its 65th birthday this past year, with a
heart-warming list of achievements; a 1,000 bed Hadassah-
Hebrew University Medical Center and the Mt. Scopus Hospital
in East Jerusalem which services people of all races and creeds.
Here teaching and research are carried on which benefit all man-
kind. Hadassah’s varied projects
Medical Organization, Youth Aliyah, Hadassah Israel Educa-
tional Services, Jewish National Fund, Zionist Youth Activities,
and American Affairs. Each of our three groups in Jacksonville
develops its own program of activities to support these projects,
with the Chapter co-ordinating the three groups.

We heartily welcome you, one and all, to join us, to get to know
us, and work with us. Please call our Chapter President, Mrs.
Joel Fleet, (Margaret) 396-7406 for further information. Mean-
while, we wish you and your dear ones a healthy and happy
year. Shalom!

River Garden Auxiliary

OFFICERS

Aging is fast becoming everyone’s business, as the
miracle of modern medicine extends the life span. This
impact on family community has become very significant
and River Garden Auxiliary is helping deal with this
situation.

In this our 50th year, the River Garden Auxiliary with
its over 900 members, pledge to continue completing
successful years of Service and devotion to our Aged, our
Family and Friends at River Garden.

All of our services do not always fit themselves into
special categories, however; some of our services both from
our membership and through financial aide can better be
understood by listing those offered. Some services are
helping to re-decorate the home, expenses toward the
repairs of both the interior and exterior, expenses for the
air-conditioning, the kitchen, infirmary equipment and
hobby shop materials.

This year as in the past our source of income is funded
through our: Tombola, Donor, Social Printing, Tree of Life
and Life Membership. The Linen Shower, always a success,
helps stock the linen closets for the residents and brings in
cash for other: needed supplies.

Jacksonville, Florida 32207

TWWWWWWWwUUW wv an oo a a to oo oo Wo WU LW LL La Lo LaLa aL Le Lee Lae

 

Programming at River Garden means more than just
Medical Services; we of the Auxiliary bring hours of
manpower, comfort and enjoyment and the knowledge that
someone cares. Through the River Garden Auxiliary we
sponsor The Red Cross Ladies, Special Holiday programs,
Friendly Visits, The River Garden Auxiliary Magazine with
articles by the residents, Ceramic Classes, Painting and Art
Classes and the Project Greenthumb. For Passover we
supply food for Raiford and MacClenny and some of the
members of the Auxiliary attend the services at Raiford
helping conduct services and distribute the food. The
Sunshine Club which is a community club for all senior
citizens and is attended by the local residents through the
drivers program of the Auxiliary.

River Garden Auxiliary attempts to render services to
our residents to fulfill a social atmosphere and the secure
knowledge that one is living at their home away from home.
All these achievements are made possible only through the
zealous efforts of the River Garden Auxiliary board and its
members.

There is always a need for members to serve on new
projects and it is our wish that each of you who read this will
take time and effort to be counted among the many and
extend a helping hand so that our River Garden Home will
always be there for you and you and — me.

Eleanor Dorman, 733-2980

Our President

When the name of the Jacksonville Jewish Center is mentioned, what
immediately comes to your mind .. . what is your first thought? Exactly
what does the center mean to you?

Do you think of the professional staff, the Solomon Schechter School,
your wedding, a Bar Mitzvah, or perhaps the funeral of a loved one? Or,
do you call to mind your annual dues, building fund pledge, committee

work or other solicitations for help from the Center? Just what do you Lawrence “Laurie” DuBow
think?

 

Are you convinced that you have given more than you have gained? No one . . . not one member of this
Congregation has given or even offered to give more than they could or should have of their time, their
money or their energy, in proportion to what the Jacksonville Jewish Center has given to each of us.

It has upgraded our past, improved and enriched the present and endowed our future. The Center has
touched and affected the lives of us all. What have you done to affect the Life of the Center . . . and is it
enough? Could you or should you do more? Certainly each of us express our commitment in a different
way, but surely everyone can become involved as an expression of their dedication. Being involved is a
way of communicating what the Center really means to you.

Remember . . . if you have confidence in what we are doing and you care enough to send us the very best
.... come yourself. 3

Linda, Susan, Michael and I send our best wishes to you all for a Happy and Healthy New Year.

Laurie

 

The DuBows, Susan, Laurie, Linda and Michael.

In Memoriam
Fran Wilensky

 

~ oe pee

(Left to right) Billy Wilensky, Danny Wilensky, Yitzhak
Rabin, and Alexander Brest with the Fran Wilensky Award.

 

Fran was founder of Mt. Carmel Gardens where she served as the
Executive Committee and as Vice-President. Many Senior Citizens
have found a happy home at Mt. Carmel and this gave Fran great
pleasure. The young also interested her. Under her capable leader-
ship a program was developed to send high school students to
study in Israel.

For many years she served the Women’s Division of U.J.A., first
as Chairman and then as Honorary Chairman. This past year, for
the first time, the Fran Wilensky Award was given to Alexander

————— : Brest for his contributions to the Jewish Community.
It is most appropriate that we honor Fran Wilensky, of blessed In April a Z.O.A. Banquet for the Jewish National Fund was held

 

memory, in the Community section of the Center Yearbook. For to honor the memory of Fran Wilensky.
it was the entire Jewish Community for which she devoted many The Jewish Community has lost a wonderful lady . . . her good
years of her life. | deeds and generosity will keep her memory alive.

The **Chug Ivri’’ of Jacksonville

Of all the links that bind our people to its past and to its present, one of the strongest is the Hebrew language. Our Hebrew tongue enables us
to display our loyalty and our commitment to our Jewish nation by “speaking its language.”

Our rabbis tell us that one of the mitzvot that enabled Israel to merit its redemption from Egyptian bondage was its having remained steadfast
and loyal to its Hebrew tongue.

Ever since that time Hebrew has been the “lashon ha-kodesh” (the holy tongue) of our people, in our sacred literature as well as a vehicle of
expression and communication among Jews, thus serving as an international language for Jews everywhere. :

It has been only during the past century or less, however, that spoken Hebrew has gained momentum among Jews. In Israel today, Hebrew
has now become the predominant language. Outside of Israel it is also winning wide popularity as a medium of expression.

Apropos of this general trend the “Chug Ivri” (the Hebrew Club) of Jacksonville is doing its part in keeping spoken Hebrew alive asa potent
and dynamic force in the life of the local Jewish community. It was nearly four years ago that this group was organized in its present form by
Shaul Baruch, then Educational Director of the Jacksonville Jewish Center and by Dr. Morris Golomb, its present President. Since that time it
has continued to function as a vehicle of expression for those interested in speaking Hebrew and meeting in delightful Hebrew atmosphere.

For this purpose the Chug Ivri meets monthly in members’ homes where “RAK IVRIT” (only Hebrew) prevails for all talks and discussions.
At each meeting one or more of the members will deliver a short talk or conduct a panel discussion on some topic of general interest. An open
discussion among those present then follows, when everyone may express himself of herself freely. The singing of Hebrew songs is also an
integral part of every Chug Ivri meeting, thereby enabling everyone to enjoy as well as learn the use of the Hebrew language. An informal
atmosphere prevails where all present get to socialize and to partake of refreshments. :

A knowledge of Hebrew is not necessary for membership, and anyone interested in furthering the advance of our Hebrew culture is invited
to join. For further information please contact Dr. Morris Golomb, President, at 733-8170 or the Center, 268-6736.
